<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en"><span dir="auto">Sinking of the RMS <i>Titanic</i></span></h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="hatnote">For the musical work by Gavin Bryars, see <a href="/wiki/The_Sinking_of_the_Titanic" title="The Sinking of the Titanic">The Sinking of the Titanic</a>.</div>
<p><span style="font-size: small;"><span id="coordinates"><a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">Coordinates</a>: <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;params=41_43_55_N_49_56_45_W_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">41°43′55″N</span> <span class="longitude">49°56′45″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">41.73194°N 49.94583°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">41.73194; -49.94583</span></span></span></a></span></span></span></p>

<p>The <b>sinking of the RMS <i>Titanic</i></b> occurred on the night of 14 April through to the morning of 15 April 1912 in the north Atlantic Ocean, four days into her <a href="/wiki/Maiden_voyage" title="Maiden voyage">maiden voyage</a> from <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a> to <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. The largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superliner_(passenger_ship)" title="Superliner (passenger ship)">passenger liner</a> in service at the time, <i><a href="/wiki/RMS_Titanic" title="RMS Titanic">Titanic</a></i> had an estimated 2,224 people on board when she struck an <a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">iceberg</a> at 23:40 (ship's time)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shiptime_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-shiptime-1"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup> on Sunday, 14 April 1912. Her sinking two hours and forty minutes later at 02:20 (05:18 <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Mean_Time" title="Greenwich Mean Time">GMT</a>) on Monday, 15 April resulted in the deaths of more than 1,500 people, which made it one of the deadliest <a href="/wiki/List_of_maritime_disasters#Peacetime_disasters" title="List of maritime disasters">peacetime maritime disasters</a> in history.</p>
<p><i>Titanic</i> received six warnings of sea ice on 14 April but was travelling near her maximum speed when her crew sighted the iceberg. Unable to turn quickly enough, the ship suffered a glancing blow that buckled her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starboard" title="Starboard">starboard</a> (right) side and opened five of her sixteen <a href="/wiki/Compartment_(ship)" title="Compartment (ship)">compartments</a> to the sea. <i>Titanic</i> had been designed to stay afloat with four of her forward compartments flooded but not more, and the crew soon realised that the ship would sink. They used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flare_(pyrotechnic)#Maritime_distress_signal" title="Flare (pyrotechnic)">rocket flares</a> and radio (<a href="/wiki/Wireless_telegraphy" title="Wireless telegraphy">wireless</a>) messages to attract help as the passengers were put into <a href="/wiki/Lifeboat_(shipboard)" title="Lifeboat (shipboard)">lifeboats</a>. However, in accordance with existing maritime practice, the ship was carrying far too few lifeboats for everyone (though slightly more than the law required), and many boats were not filled to their capacity due to a poorly managed evacuation.</p>
<p>The ship sank with over a thousand passengers and crew members still on board. Almost all those who jumped or fell into the water died from <a href="/wiki/Hypothermia" title="Hypothermia">hypothermia</a> within minutes. <a href="/wiki/RMS_Carpathia" title="RMS Carpathia">RMS <i>Carpathia</i></a> arrived on the scene about an hour and a half after the sinking and had rescued the last of the survivors in the lifeboats by 09:15 on 15 April, little more than 24 hours after <i>Titanic</i> '​s crew had received their first warnings of drifting ice. The disaster caused widespread public outrage over the lack of lifeboats, lax shipping regulations, and the unequal treatment of the different passenger classes aboard the ship. Inquiries set up in the wake of the disaster recommended sweeping changes to maritime regulations. This led to the establishment in 1914 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Convention_for_the_Safety_of_Life_at_Sea" title="International Convention for the Safety of Life at Sea">International Convention for the Safety of Life at Sea</a> (SOLAS), which still governs maritime safety today.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>At the time of her entry into service on 2 April 1912, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail_Ship" title="Royal Mail Ship">Royal Mail Ship</a> (RMS) <i>Titanic</i> was the second of three<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>b<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Olympic-class_ocean_liner" title="Olympic-class ocean liner">Olympic-class ocean liner</a> <a href="/wiki/Sister_ship" title="Sister ship">sister ships</a>, and was the largest ship in the world. She and her sister, <a href="/wiki/RMS_Olympic" title="RMS Olympic">RMS <i>Olympic</i></a>, were almost one and half times the <a href="/wiki/Gross_register_tonnage" title="Gross register tonnage">gross register tonnage</a> of Cunard's <a href="/wiki/RMS_Lusitania" title="RMS Lusitania">RMS <i>Lusitania</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/RMS_Mauretania_(1906)" title="RMS Mauretania (1906)">RMS <i>Mauretania</i></a>, the previous record holders, and were nearly 100 feet (30 m) longer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201137_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech201137-4"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Titanic</i> could carry 3,547 people in speed and comfort,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler199810_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler199810-5"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> and was built on a hitherto unprecedented scale. Her <a href="/wiki/Reciprocating_engine" title="Reciprocating engine">reciprocating engines</a> were the largest that had ever been built, standing 40 feet (12 m) high and with cylinders 9 feet (2.7 m) in diameter requiring the burning of 600 long tons (610 t) of coal per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler199810_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler199810-5"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RMS_Titanic_sea_trials_April_2,_1912.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1050" data-file-width="1393" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/RMS_Titanic_sea_trials_April_2%2C_1912.jpg/220px-RMS_Titanic_sea_trials_April_2%2C_1912.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/RMS_Titanic_sea_trials_April_2%2C_1912.jpg/330px-RMS_Titanic_sea_trials_April_2%2C_1912.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/RMS_Titanic_sea_trials_April_2%2C_1912.jpg/440px-RMS_Titanic_sea_trials_April_2%2C_1912.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RMS_Titanic_sea_trials_April_2,_1912.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Titanic</i> on her <a href="/wiki/Sea_trial" title="Sea trial">sea trials</a>, 2 April 1912</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Her passenger accommodation was said to be "of unrivalled extent and magnificence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler199816.E2.80.9320_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler199816.E2.80.9320-6"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/First_class_facilities_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="First class facilities of the RMS Titanic">First Class accommodation</a> included the most expensive seagoing real estate ever, with <a href="/wiki/Promenade_deck" title="Promenade deck">promenade</a> suites costing $4,350 (equivalent to $106,310 today)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-7"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> for a one-way passage. Even Third Class was unusually comfortable by contemporary standards and was supplied with plentiful quantities of good food, providing its passengers with better conditions than many of them had experienced at home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler199816.E2.80.9320_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler199816.E2.80.9320-6"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Titanic</i> '​s maiden voyage began shortly after noon on 10 April 1912 when she left Southampton on the first leg of her journey to New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201167_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201167-8"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> A few hours later she reached <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cherbourg" title="Cherbourg">Cherbourg</a> in France, a journey of 80 nautical miles (148 km; 92 mi), where she took on passengers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201171_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201171-9"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Her next port of call was Queenstown (now <a href="/wiki/Cobh" title="Cobh">Cobh</a>) in Ireland, which she reached around midday on 11 April.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201176_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201176-10"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> She left in the afternoon after taking on more passengers and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ship%27s_stores" title="Ship's stores">stores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201177_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201177-11"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By the time she departed westwards across the Atlantic she was carrying 892 crew members and 1,320 passengers. This was only about half of her full passenger capacity of 2,435,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998238_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998238-12"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> as it was the low season and shipping from the UK had been disrupted by a <a href="/wiki/National_coal_strike_of_1912" title="National coal strike of 1912">coal miners' strike</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord198783_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord198783-13"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Her passengers were a cross-section of <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian</a> society, from millionaires such as <a href="/wiki/John_Jacob_Astor_IV" title="John Jacob Astor IV">John Jacob Astor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Guggenheim" title="Benjamin Guggenheim">Benjamin Guggenheim</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler199827.E2.80.938_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler199827.E2.80.938-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> to poor emigrants from countries as disparate as Armenia, Ireland, Italy, Sweden, Syria and Russia seeking a new life in America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowells199995_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowells199995-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_voyage_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="666" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Titanic_voyage_map.png/350px-Titanic_voyage_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Titanic_voyage_map.png/525px-Titanic_voyage_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/51/Titanic_voyage_map.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_voyage_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Route of <i>Titanic</i> '​s maiden voyage from <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a> to <a href="/wiki/New_York" title="New York">New York</a> with the point where she sank (marked in gold/yellow)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The ship was commanded by 62-year-old Captain <a href="/wiki/Edward_Smith_(sea_captain)" title="Edward Smith (sea captain)">Edward John Smith</a>, the most senior of the <a href="/wiki/White_Star_Line" title="White Star Line">White Star Line</a>'s captains. He had four decades of seafaring experience and had previously served as captain of the <a href="/wiki/RMS_Olympic" title="RMS Olympic">RMS <i>Olympic</i></a>, from which he was transferred to command <i>Titanic</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201143.E2.80.934_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201143.E2.80.934-16"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> The vast majority of the crew who served under him were not trained sailors, but were either engineers, firemen, or stokers, responsible for looking after the engines; or stewards and galley staff, responsible for the passengers. The 6 watch officers and 39 <a href="/wiki/Able_seaman" title="Able seaman">able-bodied seamen</a> constituted only around 5 percent of the crew,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998238_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998238-12"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> and most of these had been taken on at Southampton so had not had time to familiarise themselves with the ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201149_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201149-17"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The ice conditions were attributed to a mild winter that caused large numbers of icebergs to shift off the west coast of Greenland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan19858_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan19858-18"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition, it is now known that in January 1912, the Moon came closer to the Earth than at any time in the previous 1,400 years, at the same time as the Earth made its closest annual approach to the Sun. This caused exceptionally high tides that may have resulted in a larger number of icebergs than usual reaching the shipping lanes a few months later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlsonDoescherSinnott201234.E2.80.939_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlsonDoescherSinnott201234.E2.80.939-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroad2012_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroad2012-20"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> The weather improved significantly during the course of the day, from brisk winds and moderate seas in the morning to a crystal-clear calm by evening, as the ship entered an arctic high pressure system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpern201180_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpern201180-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> There was no moon on the clear night.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="14_April_1912">14 April 1912</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 14 April 1912">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iceberg_warnings_.2809:00.E2.80.9323:39.29">Iceberg warnings (09:00–23:39)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Iceberg warnings (09:00–23:39)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_iceberg.jpg"><img alt='Black and white photograph of a large iceberg with three "peaks".' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="740" data-file-width="1308" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Titanic_iceberg.jpg/220px-Titanic_iceberg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Titanic_iceberg.jpg/330px-Titanic_iceberg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Titanic_iceberg.jpg/440px-Titanic_iceberg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_iceberg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The iceberg thought to have been hit by <i>Titanic</i>, photographed by the chief steward of the liner <a href="/wiki/SS_Prinz_Adalbert" title="SS Prinz Adalbert"><i>Prinz Adalbert</i></a> on the morning of 15 April 1912. The iceberg was reported to have a streak of red paint from a ship's hull along its waterline on one side.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During 14 April 1912, <i>Titanic</i> '​s radio<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wireless_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-wireless-22"><span>[</span>c<span>]</span></a></sup> operators received six messages from other ships warning of drifting ice, which passengers on <i>Titanic</i> had begun to notice during the afternoon. The ice conditions in the North Atlantic were the worst for any April in the previous 50 years (which was the reason why the lookouts were unaware that they were about to steam into a line of drifting ice several miles wide and many miles long).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987199_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987199-23"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Not all of these messages were relayed by the radio operators. At the time, all wireless operators on ocean liners were employees of the <a href="/wiki/Marconi_Company" title="Marconi Company">Marconi Company</a> and not members of their ship's crew; their primary responsibility was to send messages for the passengers, with weather reports as a secondary concern.</p>
<p>The first warning came at 09:00 from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RMS_Caronia_(1905)" title="RMS Caronia (1905)">RMS <i>Caronia</i></a> reporting "bergs, growlers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-growler_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-growler-24"><span>[</span>d<span>]</span></a></sup> and field ice".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan19859_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan19859-25"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Captain Smith acknowledged receipt of the message. At 13:42, <a href="/wiki/RMS_Baltic_(1903)" title="RMS Baltic (1903)">RMS <i>Baltic</i></a> relayed a report from the Greek ship <i>Athenia</i> that she had been "passing icebergs and large quantities of field ice".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan19859_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan19859-25"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> This too was acknowledged by Smith, who showed the report to <a href="/wiki/J._Bruce_Ismay" title="J. Bruce Ismay">J. Bruce Ismay</a>, the chairman of the White Star Line, aboard <i>Titanic</i> for her maiden voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan19859_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan19859-25"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Smith ordered a new course to be set, to take the ship farther south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006191_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006191-26"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At 13:45, the German ship <a href="/wiki/USS_America_(ID-3006)" title="USS America (ID-3006)">SS <i>Amerika</i></a>, which was a short distance to the south, reported she had "passed two large icebergs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan198510_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan198510-27"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> This message never reached Captain Smith or the other officers on <i>Titanic</i> '​s <a href="/wiki/Bridge_(nautical)" title="Bridge (nautical)">bridge</a>. The reason is unclear, but it may have been forgotten because the radio operators had to fix faulty equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan198510_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan198510-27"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/SS_Californian" title="SS Californian">SS <i>Californian</i></a> reported "three large bergs" at 19:30, and at 21:40, the steamer <i>Mesaba</i> reported: "Saw much heavy <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">pack ice</a> and great number large icebergs. Also field ice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan198511_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan198511-28"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> This message, too, never left the <i>Titanic's</i> radio room. The radio operator, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Phillips_(wireless_officer)" title="Jack Phillips (wireless officer)">Jack Phillips</a>, may have failed to grasp its significance because he was preoccupied with transmitting messages for passengers via the relay station at <a href="/wiki/Cape_Race" title="Cape Race">Cape Race</a>, Newfoundland; the radio set had broken down the day before, resulting in a backlog of messages that the two operators were trying to clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan198510_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan198510-27"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> A final warning was received at 22:30 from operator <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Furmstone_Evans" title="Cyril Furmstone Evans">Cyril Evans</a> of the <i>Californian</i>, which had halted for the night in an ice field some miles away, but Phillips cut it off and signalled back: "Shut up! Shut up! I'm working Cape Race."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan198511_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan198511-28"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although the crew was aware of ice in the vicinity, the ship's speed was not reduced, and she continued to steam at 22 knots (41 km/h; 25 mph), only 2 knots (3.7 km/h; 2.3 mph) short of her maximum speed of 24 knots (44 km/h; 28 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan198510_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan198510-27"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>e<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Titanic</i> '​s high speed in waters where ice had been reported was later criticised as reckless, but it reflected standard maritime practice at the time. According to Fifth Officer <a href="/wiki/Harold_Lowe" title="Harold Lowe">Harold Lowe</a>, the custom was "to go ahead and depend upon the lookouts in the crow's nest and the watch on the bridge to pick up the ice in time to avoid hitting it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMowbray1912278_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMowbray1912278-31"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The North Atlantic liners prioritised time-keeping above all other considerations, sticking rigidly to a schedule that would guarantee their arrival at an advertised time. They were frequently driven at close to their full speed, treating hazard warnings as advisories rather than calls to action. It was widely believed that ice posed little risk; close calls were not uncommon, and even head-on collisions had not been disastrous. In 1907 <a href="/wiki/SS_Kronprinz_Wilhelm" title="SS Kronprinz Wilhelm">SS <i>Kronprinz Wilhelm</i></a>, a German liner, had rammed an iceberg and suffered a crushed bow, but was still able to complete her voyage. That same year, <i>Titanic</i> '​s future captain, Edward Smith, declared in an interview that he could not "imagine any condition which would cause a ship to founder. Modern shipbuilding has gone beyond that."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200613_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200613-32"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Iceberg_right_ahead.21.22_.2823:39.29">"Iceberg right ahead!" (23:39)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title='Edit section: "Iceberg right ahead!" (23:39)'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Collision">Collision</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Collision">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote boilerplate seealso">For more details on the missing binoculars, see <a href="/wiki/David_Blair_(mariner)" title="David Blair (mariner)">David Blair (mariner)</a>.</div>
<p>As <i>Titanic</i> approached her fatal crash, most passengers had gone to bed and command of the bridge had passed from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Officer_(nautical)" title="Second Officer (nautical)">Second Officer</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lightoller" title="Charles Lightoller">Charles Lightoller</a> to <a href="/wiki/Chief_mate" title="Chief mate">First Officer</a> <a href="/wiki/William_McMaster_Murdoch" title="William McMaster Murdoch">William Murdoch</a>. Lookouts <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Fleet" title="Frederick Fleet">Frederick Fleet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Lee" title="Reginald Lee">Reginald Lee</a> were occupying the <a href="/wiki/Crow%27s_nest" title="Crow's nest">crow's nest</a> 29 metres (95 ft) above the deck. The air temperature had fallen to near freezing and the ocean was completely calm. Colonel <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Gracie_IV" title="Archibald Gracie IV">Archibald Gracie</a>, one of the survivors of the disaster, later wrote that "the sea was like glass, so smooth that the stars were clearly reflected."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGracie1913247_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGracie1913247-33"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> It is now known that such exceptionally calm water is a sign of nearby pack ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpern201185_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpern201185-34"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although the air was clear, there was no moon, and with the sea so calm, there was nothing to give away the position of the nearby icebergs; had the sea been rougher, waves breaking against the icebergs would have made them more visible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas198719_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas198719-35"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Because of a mix-up at Southampton the lookouts had no binoculars; but reportedly binoculars would not have been effective in darkness which was total except for starlight and the ship's own lights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown200047_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown200047-36"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> The lookouts were nonetheless well aware of the ice hazard, as Lightoller had ordered them and other crew members to "keep a sharp look-out for ice, particularly small ice and growlers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-growler_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-growler-24"><span>[</span>d<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarratt2010122_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarratt2010122-37"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_porting_around_English.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="449" data-file-width="1130" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Titanic_porting_around_English.svg/500px-Titanic_porting_around_English.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Titanic_porting_around_English.svg/750px-Titanic_porting_around_English.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Titanic_porting_around_English.svg/1000px-Titanic_porting_around_English.svg.png 2x" width="500"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_porting_around_English.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diagram of <i>Titanic</i> '​s course at the time of the collision with the iceberg.<br/>
(Blue: path of bow. Red: path of stern.)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At 23:30, Fleet and Lee noticed a slight haze on the horizon ahead of them, but did not make anything of it. Nine minutes later, at 23:39, Fleet spotted an iceberg in <i>Titanic</i> '​s path. He rang the lookout bell three times and telephoned the bridge to inform Sixth Officer <a href="/wiki/James_Paul_Moody" title="James Paul Moody">James Moody</a>. Fleet asked "Is there anyone there?" Moody replied, "Yes, what do you see?" Fleet replied: "Iceberg, right ahead!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord20052_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord20052-38"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> After thanking Fleet, Moody relayed the message to Murdoch, who ordered Quartermaster <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Hichens_(RMS_Titanic)" title="Robert Hichens (RMS Titanic)">Robert Hichens</a> to change the ship's course.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994137_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994137-39"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Murdoch is generally believed to have given the order "Hard a'starboard" which would result in the ship's <a href="/wiki/Tiller" title="Tiller">tiller</a> being moved all the way to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starboard" title="Starboard">starboard</a> (the right side of the ship) in an attempt to turn the ship to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_(nautical)" title="Port (nautical)">port</a> (left).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown200047_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown200047-36"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> He also rang "Full Astern" on the <a href="/wiki/Engine_order_telegraph" title="Engine order telegraph">ship's telegraphs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006191_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006191-26"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>According to Fourth Officer <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Boxhall" title="Joseph Boxhall">Joseph Boxhall</a>, Murdoch told Captain Smith that he was attempting to "hard-a-port around [the iceberg]", suggesting that he was attempting a "port around" manoeuvre – to first swing the bow around the obstacle, then swing the stern so that both ends of the ship would avoid a collision. There was a delay before either order went into effect; the steam-powered steering mechanism took up to 30 seconds to turn the ship's tiller,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006191_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006191-26"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> and the complex task of setting the engines into reverse would also have taken some time to accomplish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown200067_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown200067-40"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Because the centre turbine could not be reversed, both it and the centre propeller, positioned directly in front of the ship's rudder, were simply stopped. This reduced the rudder's effectiveness, therefore impairing the turning ability of the ship. Had Murdoch simply turned the ship while maintaining her forward speed, <i>Titanic</i> might have missed the iceberg with feet to spare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006194_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006194-41"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the event, <i>Titanic</i> '​s heading changed just in time to avoid a head-on collision, but the change in direction caused the ship to strike the iceberg with a glancing blow. An underwater spur of ice scraped along the starboard side of the ship for about seven seconds; chunks of ice dislodged from upper parts of the berg fell onto her forward decks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011100_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011100-42"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> A few minutes later, all of <i>Titanic</i> '​s engines were stopped, leaving the ship facing north and drifting in the <a href="/wiki/Labrador_Current" title="Labrador Current">Labrador Current</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpern201194_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpern201194-43"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_the_collision">Effects of the collision</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Effects of the collision">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iceberg_and_titanic_(en).svg"><img alt="Diagram showing how the iceberg buckled Titanic '​s hull, causing the riveted plates to come apart." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="382" data-file-width="511" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Iceberg_and_titanic_%28en%29.svg/220px-Iceberg_and_titanic_%28en%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Iceberg_and_titanic_%28en%29.svg/330px-Iceberg_and_titanic_%28en%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Iceberg_and_titanic_%28en%29.svg/440px-Iceberg_and_titanic_%28en%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iceberg_and_titanic_(en).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The iceberg buckled the plates, popping rivets and damaging a sequence of compartments. Contrary to widespread assumption, the iceberg did not slice the hull.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The impact with the iceberg was long thought to have produced a huge opening in <i>Titanic</i> '​s hull, "not less than 300 feet (91 m) in length, 10 feet (3.0 m) above the level of the keel", as one writer later put it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoffmanGrimm198220_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoffmanGrimm198220-44"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> However, at the British enquiry following the accident, Edward Wilding (chief naval architect for Harland and Wolff), calculating on the basis of the observed flooding of forward compartments forty minutes after the collision, testified that the area of the hull opened to the sea was "somewhere about 12 square feet".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilding_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilding-45"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> He also stated that "I believe it must have been in places, not a continuous rip", but that the different openings must have extended along an area of around 300 feet, to account for the flooding in several compartments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilding_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-wilding-45"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> However, the official findings of the enquiry simply state that the damage extended about 300 feet, and hence many subsequent writers followed this statement. Modern <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasound</a> surveys of the <a href="/wiki/Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Wreck of the RMS Titanic">wreck</a> have found that the damage consisted of six narrow openings in an area of the hull covering only about 12 to 13 square feet (1.1 to 1.2 m<sup>2</sup>) in total. According to Paul K. Matthias, who made the measurements, the damage consisted of a "series of deformations in the starboard side that start and stop along the hull ... about 10 feet [3.0 m] above the bottom of the ship.".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroad1997_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroad1997-46"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The gaps, the longest of which measures about 39 feet (12 m) long, appear to have followed the line of the hull plates. This suggests that the iron rivets along the plate seams snapped off or popped open to create narrow gaps through which water flooded. An engineer from <i>Titanic</i> '​s builders, <a href="/wiki/Harland_and_Wolff" title="Harland and Wolff">Harland and Wolff</a>, suggested this scenario at <a href="/wiki/British_Wreck_Commissioner%27s_inquiry_into_the_sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="British Wreck Commissioner's inquiry into the sinking of the RMS Titanic">the British Wreck Commissioner's inquiry</a> following the disaster but his view was discounted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroad1997_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroad1997-46"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Titanic</i> '​s discoverer <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ballard" title="Robert Ballard">Robert Ballard</a> has commented that the assumption that the ship had suffered a massive breach was "a byproduct of the mystique of the <i>Titanic</i>. No one could believe that the great ship was sunk by a little sliver."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198725_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198725-47"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> Faults in the ship's hull may have been a contributing factor. Recovered pieces of <i>Titanic</i> '​s hull plates appear to have shattered on impact with the iceberg, without bending.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2008457_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2008457-48"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The plates in the central 60% of the hull were held together with triple rows of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mild_steel" title="Mild steel">mild steel</a> <a href="/wiki/Rivet" title="Rivet">rivets</a>, but the plates in the bow and stern were held together with double rows of <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought iron</a> rivets which were – according to materials scientists <a href="/wiki/Tim_Foecke" title="Tim Foecke">Tim Foecke</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jennifer_McCarty&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jennifer McCarty (page does not exist)">Jennifer McCarty</a> – near their <a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)" title="Stress (mechanics)">stress</a> limits even before the collision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Materials_Today.27.27.2C_2008_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Materials_Today.27.27.2C_2008-49"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCartyFoecke201283_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCartyFoecke201283-50"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> These "Best" or No. 3 iron rivets had a high level of slag inclusions, making them more brittle than the more usual "Best-Best" No. 4 iron rivets, and more prone to snapping when put under stress, particularly in extreme cold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroad2008_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroad2008-51"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerhoeven200749_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerhoeven200749-52"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> But Tom McCluskie, a retired archivist of Harland &amp; Wolff, pointed out that <i><a href="/wiki/RMS_Olympic" title="RMS Olympic">Olympic</a></i>, <i>Titanic'</i>s sister ship, was riveted with the same iron and served without incident for nearly 25 years, surviving several major collisions, including being rammed by a British cruiser.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEwers2008_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEwers2008-53"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> The <i>Olympic</i> even rammed and sank the U-boat <i><a href="/wiki/SM_U-103" title="SM U-103">U-103</a></i> with her bow. Thereby, the stem was twisted and hull plates on the starboard side were buckled without impairing the hull's integrity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEwers2008_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEwers2008-53"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills199346_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills199346-54"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Above the waterline, there was little evidence of the collision. The stewards in the first class dining room noticed a shudder, which they thought might have been caused by the ship shedding a propeller blade. Many of the passengers felt a bump or shudder but did not know what it was.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler199867.E2.80.939_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler199867.E2.80.939-55"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Those on the lowest decks, nearest the site of the collision, felt it much more directly. Engine Oiler Walter Hurst recalled being "awakened by a grinding crash along the starboard side. No one was very much alarmed but knew we had struck something".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarratt2010151_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarratt2010151-56"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fireman_(steam_engine)" title="Fireman (steam engine)">Fireman</a> George Kemish heard a "heavy thud and grinding tearing sound" from the starboard hull.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarratt2010156_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarratt2010156-57"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The ship began to flood immediately, with water pouring in at an estimated rate of 7 long tons (7.1 t)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> per second, fifteen times faster than it could be pumped out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAldridge200886_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAldridge200886-59"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Second Engineer J. H. Hesketh and Leading Stoker Frederick Barrett were hit by a jet of icy water in No. 6 boiler room and escaped just before the room's watertight door closed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198771_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198771-60"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> This was an extremely dangerous situation for the engineering staff; the boilers were still full of hot high-pressure steam and there was a substantial risk that they would explode if they came into contact with the cold seawater flooding the boiler rooms. The stokers and firemen were ordered to reduce the fires and vent the boilers, sending great quantities of steam up the funnel venting pipes. They were waist-deep in freezing water by the time they finished their work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200618_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200618-61"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:702px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_side_plan_annotated_English.png"><img alt="A line diagram showing Titanic from the side." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1180" data-file-width="3735" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Titanic_side_plan_annotated_English.png/700px-Titanic_side_plan_annotated_English.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Titanic_side_plan_annotated_English.png/1050px-Titanic_side_plan_annotated_English.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Titanic_side_plan_annotated_English.png/1400px-Titanic_side_plan_annotated_English.png 2x" width="700"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_side_plan_annotated_English.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Annotated diagram of RMS <i>Titanic</i> showing the arrangement of the bulkheads. The areas of damage are shown in green. The compartments in the engineering area at the bottom of the ship are noted in blue. The scale's smallest unit is 10 feet (3.0 m) and its total length is 400 feet (120 m).</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Titanic</i> '​s lower decks were divided into sixteen <a href="/wiki/Compartment_(ship)" title="Compartment (ship)">compartments</a>. Each was separated from its neighbour by a bulkhead running the width of the ship; there were fifteen bulkheads in all. Every bulkhead extended at least to the underside of E Deck, nominally one deck, or about 11 feet (3.4 m), above the waterline. The two nearest the bow and the six nearest the stern went one deck further up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMersey1912_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMersey1912-62"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Each bulkhead could be sealed by watertight doors. The engine rooms and boiler rooms on the tank top deck had vertically closing doors that could be controlled remotely from the bridge, lowered automatically by a float if water was present, or closed manually by the crew. These took about 30 seconds to close; warning bells and alternate escape routes were provided so that the crew would not be trapped by the doors. Above the tank top level, on the Orlop Deck, F Deck and E Deck, the doors closed horizontally and were manually operated. They could be closed at the door itself or from the deck above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMersey1912_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMersey1912-62"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although the watertight bulkheads extended well above the water line, they were not sealed at the top. If too many compartments were flooded, the ship's bow would settle deeper in the water, and water would spill from one compartment to the next in sequence, rather like water spilling across the top of an ice cube tray. This was what happened to <i>Titanic</i>, which had suffered damage to the forepeak tank, the three forward holds and No. 6 boiler room, a total of five compartments. <i>Titanic</i> was only designed to float with any <i>two</i> compartments flooded, but it could remain afloat with certain combinations of three or even four compartments (the first four) open to the ocean. With five compartments, however, the tops of the bulkheads would be submerged and the ship would continue to flood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMersey1912_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMersey1912-62"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198722_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198722-63"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanic-sinking-animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="300" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Titanic-sinking-animation.gif/220px-Titanic-sinking-animation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/27/Titanic-sinking-animation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/27/Titanic-sinking-animation.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanic-sinking-animation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Animation showing the sequence of <i>Titanic '​s</i> sinking</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Captain Smith felt the collision in his cabin and came immediately to the bridge. Informed of the situation, he summoned <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Andrews_(shipbuilder)" title="Thomas Andrews (shipbuilder)">Thomas Andrews</a>, <i>Titanic '​s</i> builder, who was among a party of engineers from Harland and Wolff observing the ship's first passenger voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006147_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006147-64"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> The ship was listing five degrees to starboard and was two degrees down by the head within only a few minutes of the collision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler199871_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler199871-65"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Smith and Andrews went below and found that the forward cargo holds, the mailroom and the squash court were flooded, while No. 6 boiler room was already filled to a depth of 14 feet (4.3 m). Water was spilling over into No. 5 boiler room,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler199871_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler199871-65"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> and crewmen there were battling to pump it out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler199872_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler199872-66"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Within only 45 minutes of the collision, at least 13,500 long tons (13,700 t) of water had entered the ship. This was far too much for <i>Titanic '​s</i> ballast and bilge pumps to handle; the total pumping capacity of all the pumps combined was only 1,700 long tons (1,700 t) per hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011112_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011112-67"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> Seawater was pouring into <i>Titanic</i> 15 times faster than it could be pumped out. Andrews informed the captain that the first five compartments were flooded, and therefore <i>Titanic</i> was doomed. By his estimate, she could remain afloat for no longer than about two hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006148_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006148-68"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>From the time of the collision to the moment of her sinking, at least 35,000 long tons (36,000 t) of water flooded into <i>Titanic</i>, causing her <a href="/wiki/Displacement_(ship)" title="Displacement (ship)">displacement</a> to nearly double from 48,300 long tons (49,100 t) to over 83,000 long tons (84,000 t).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011106_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011106-69"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> The flooding did not proceed at a constant pace, nor was it distributed evenly throughout the ship, due to the configuration of the flooded compartments. Her initial list to starboard was caused by asymmetrical flooding of the starboard side as water poured down a passageway at the bottom of the ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011116_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011116-70"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> When the passageway was fully flooded, the list corrected itself but the ship later began to list to port by up to ten degrees as that side also flooded asymmetrically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011118_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011118-71"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Titanic '​s</i> down angle altered fairly rapidly from zero degrees to about four and a half degrees during the first hour after the collision, but the rate at which the ship went down slowed greatly for the second hour, worsening only to about five degrees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011109_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011109-72"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> This gave many of those aboard a false sense of hope that the ship might stay afloat long enough for them to be rescued. By 1:30, however, the sinking rate of the front section increased until <i>Titanic</i> reached a down angle of about ten degrees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011118_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011118-71"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="15_April_1912">15 April 1912</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 15 April 1912">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparing_to_evacuate_.2800:05.E2.80.9300:45.29">Preparing to evacuate (00:05–00:45)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Preparing to evacuate (00:05–00:45)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EJ_Smith.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of a bearded man wearing a white captain's uniform, standing on a ship with his arms crossed." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="545" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/EJ_Smith.jpg/220px-EJ_Smith.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/EJ_Smith.jpg/330px-EJ_Smith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/EJ_Smith.jpg/440px-EJ_Smith.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EJ_Smith.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Edward_Smith_(sea_captain)" title="Edward Smith (sea captain)">Edward J. Smith</a>, captain of <i>Titanic</i>, in 1911</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At 00:05 on 15 April, Captain Smith ordered the ship's lifeboats uncovered and the passengers mustered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198722_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198722-63"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> He also ordered the radio operators to begin sending distress calls, which wrongly placed the ship on the west side of the ice belt and directed rescuers to a position that turned out to be inaccurate by about 13.5 nautical miles (15.5 mi / 25 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987199_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987199-23"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011120_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011120-73"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Below decks, water was pouring into the lowest levels of the ship. As the mail room flooded, the mail sorters made an ultimately futile attempt to save the 400,000 items of mail being carried aboard <i>Titanic</i>. Elsewhere, air could be heard being forced out by inrushing water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011118.E2.80.939_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011118.E2.80.939-74"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> Above them, stewards went door to door, rousing sleeping passengers and crew – <i>Titanic</i> did not have a public address system – and told them to go to the Boat Deck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200620_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200620-75"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The thoroughness of the muster was heavily dependent on the class of the passengers; the first-class stewards were in charge of only a few cabins, while those responsible for the second- and third-class passengers had to manage large numbers of people. The first-class stewards provided hands-on assistance, helping their charges to get dressed and bringing them out onto the deck. With far more people to deal with, the second- and third-class stewards mostly confined their efforts to throwing open doors and telling passengers to put on lifebelts and come up top. In third class, passengers were largely left to their own devices after being informed of the need to come on deck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011121_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011121-76"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many passengers and crew were reluctant to comply, either refusing to believe that there was a problem or preferring the warmth of the ship's interior to the bitterly cold night air. The passengers were not told that the ship was sinking, though a few noticed that she was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_(watercraft)" title="List (watercraft)">listing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200620_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200620-75"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Around 00:15, the stewards began ordering the passengers to put on their lifebelts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011126_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011126-77"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> though again, many passengers took the order as a joke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200620_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200620-75"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Some set about playing an impromptu game of football (soccer) with the ice chunks that were now strewn across the foredeck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011116_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011116-78"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On the boat deck, as the crew began preparing the lifeboats, it was difficult to hear anything over the noise of high-pressure steam being vented from the boilers and escaping via the valves on the funnels above. Lawrence Beesley described the sound as "a harsh, deafening boom that made conversation difficult; if one imagines 20 locomotives blowing off steam in a low key it would give some idea of the unpleasant sound that met us as we climbed out on the top deck."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeesley196032.E2.80.933_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeesley196032.E2.80.933-79"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> The noise was so loud that the crew had to use hand signals to communicate on the deck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011124_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011124-80"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Captain Smith was faced with the fact that there were too few lifeboats to save everyone on board. <i>Titanic</i> had a total of 20 lifeboats, comprising 16 wooden boats on <a href="/wiki/Davit" title="Davit">davits</a>, 8 on either side of the ship, and 4 collapsible boats with wooden bottoms and canvas sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200620_75-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200620-75"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> The collapsibles were stored upside down with the sides folded in, and would have to be erected and moved to the davits for launching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord198790_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord198790-81"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> Two were stored under the wooden boats and the other two were lashed atop the officers' quarters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200621_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200621-82"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> The position of the latter would make them extremely difficult to launch, as they weighed several tons each and had to be manhandled down to the boat deck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011123_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011123-83"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On average, the lifeboats could take up to 68 people each, and collectively they could accommodate 1,178 – barely half the number of people on board and only a third of the number the ship was licensed to carry. The shortage of lifeboats was not because of a lack of space nor because of cost. <i>Titanic</i> had been designed to accommodate up to 68 lifeboats<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011112_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011112-84"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> – enough for everyone on board – and the price of an extra 32 lifeboats would only have been some $16,000, a tiny fraction of the $7.5 million that the company had spent on <i>Titanic</i>. In an emergency, lifeboats at the time were intended to be used to transfer passengers off the ship and onto a nearby vessel providing assistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011116_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchingsde_Kerbrech2011116-85"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>f<span>]</span></a></sup> It was therefore commonplace for liners to have far fewer lifeboats than needed to accommodate all their passengers and crew, and of the 39 British liners of the time of over 10,000 long tons (10,000 t), 33 had too few lifeboat places to accommodate everyone on board.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett201130_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett201130-88"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> The White Star Line desired the ship to have a wide promenade deck with uninterrupted views of the sea, which would have been obstructed by a continuous row of lifeboats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall1912141_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall1912141-89"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> As inadequate as the supply of lifeboats on <i>Titanic</i> was, it was still more than the outdated British regulations required, and it had simply never been envisaged that all of the crew and passengers would have to be evacuated at once, as the ship was considered almost unsinkable.</p>
<p>Captain Smith was an experienced sailor who had served for 40 years at sea, including 27 years in command. He would certainly have known that even if all the boats were fully occupied, a thousand people would remain on the ship as she went down with little chance of rescue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198722_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198722-63"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> As Smith began to grasp the enormity of what was about to happen, he appeared to have become paralysed by indecision. He did not issue a general call for evacuation, failed to order his officers to load the lifeboats, did not adequately organise the crew, withheld crucial information from his officers and crewmen, and sometimes gave ambiguous or impractical orders. Even some of his bridge officers were unaware for some time after the collision that the ship was sinking; Fourth Officer <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Boxhall" title="Joseph Boxhall">Joseph Boxhall</a> did not find out until 01:15, barely an hour before the ship went down,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998250.E2.80.932_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998250.E2.80.932-90"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> while Quartermaster George Rowe was so unaware of the emergency that after the evacuation had started, he phoned the bridge from his watch station to ask why he had just seen a lifeboat go past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011106_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011106-91"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> Smith did not advise his officers that the ship did not have enough lifeboats to save everyone. He did not supervise the loading of the lifeboats and seemingly made no effort to find out if his orders were being followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998250.E2.80.932_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998250.E2.80.932-90"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox199950.E2.80.932_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox199950.E2.80.932-92"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The crew was likewise unprepared for the emergency, as lifeboat training had been minimal. Only one <a href="/wiki/Muster_drill" title="Muster drill">lifeboat drill</a> had been conducted while the ship was docked at Southampton. It was a cursory effort, consisting of two boats being lowered, each manned by one officer and four men who merely rowed around the dock for a few minutes before returning to the ship. The boats were supposed to be stocked with emergency supplies, but <i>Titanic</i> '​s passengers later found that they had only been partially provisioned despite the best efforts of the ship's chief baker, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Joughin" title="Charles Joughin">Charles Joughin</a> and his staff to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMowbray1912279_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMowbray1912279-93"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> No lifeboat or fire drills had been conducted since <i>Titanic</i> left Southampton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMowbray1912279_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMowbray1912279-93"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> A lifeboat drill had been scheduled for the Sunday morning before the ship sank, but was cancelled for unknown reasons by Captain Smith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAldridge200847_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAldridge200847-94"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Lists had been posted on the ship assigning crew members to specific lifeboat stations, but few appeared to have read them or to have known what they were supposed to do. Most of the crew were, in any case, not seamen, and even some of those had no prior experience of rowing a boat. They were now faced with the complex task of coordinating the lowering of 20 boats carrying a possible total of 1,100 people 70 feet (21 m) down the sides of the ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011123_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011123-83"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Thomas E. Bonsall, a historian of the disaster, has commented that the evacuation was so badly organised that "even if they had the number [of] lifeboats they needed, it is impossible to see how they could have launched them" given the lack of time and poor leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox199952_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox199952-95"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By about 00:20, 40 minutes after the collision, the loading of the lifeboats was under way, though it was perhaps symptomatic of Captain Smith's apparent indecisiveness that it was at the suggestion of Second Officer Lightoller. The latter recalled afterwards that he noticed Smith standing near the bridge looking out at the ocean in a trance-like daze. According to Lightoller, "I yelled at the top of my voice, 'Hadn't we better get the women and children into the boats, sir?' He heard me and nodded reply."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleicher200665_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleicher200665-96"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> Smith then ordered Lightoller to "put the women and children in and lower away".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord200537_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord200537-97"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Lightoller took charge of the boats on the port side while Murdoch took charge of those on the starboard side. The two officers interpreted the evacuation order differently; Murdoch took it to mean <a href="/wiki/Women_and_children_first" title="Women and children first">women and children <i>first</i></a> while Lightoller took it to mean women and children <i>only</i>. Lightoller lowered lifeboats with empty seats if there were no women and children waiting to board, while Murdoch allowed a limited number of men to board if all the nearby women and children had embarked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200621_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200621-82"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Neither officer knew how many people could safely be carried in the boats as they were lowered and they both erred on the side of caution by not filling them. They could have been lowered quite safely with their full complement of 68 people, especially with the highly-favourable weather and sea conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200621_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200621-82"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Had this been done, an extra 500 people could have been saved; instead, hundreds of people, predominantly men, were left on board as lifeboats were launched with many seats vacant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011124_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011124-80"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox199952_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox199952-95"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Few passengers at first were willing to board the lifeboats and the officers in charge of the evacuation found it difficult to persuade them. The millionaire <a href="/wiki/John_Jacob_Astor_IV" title="John Jacob Astor IV">John Jacob Astor</a> declared: "We are safer here than in that little boat."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord197673.E2.80.934_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord197673.E2.80.934-98"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> Some passengers refused flatly to embark. J. Bruce Ismay, realising the need for urgency, roamed the starboard deck and urged passengers and crew to evacuate. A trickle of women, couples and single men were persuaded to board starboard lifeboat No. 7, which became the first lifeboat to be lowered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord197673.E2.80.934_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord197673.E2.80.934-98"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Departure_of_the_lifeboats_.2800:45.E2.80.9302:05.29">Departure of the lifeboats (00:45–02:05)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Departure of the lifeboats (00:45–02:05)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Lifeboats_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Lifeboats of the RMS Titanic">Lifeboats of the RMS Titanic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Sad_Parting_-_no_caption.jpg"><img alt="Illustration of a weeping woman being comforted by a man on the sloping deck of a ship. In the background men are loading other women into a lifeboat." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="945" data-file-width="617" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/The_Sad_Parting_-_no_caption.jpg/170px-The_Sad_Parting_-_no_caption.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/The_Sad_Parting_-_no_caption.jpg/255px-The_Sad_Parting_-_no_caption.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/The_Sad_Parting_-_no_caption.jpg/340px-The_Sad_Parting_-_no_caption.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Sad_Parting_-_no_caption.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>"The Sad Parting"</i>, illustration of 1912</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At 00:45 lifeboat No. 7 was rowed away from <i>Titanic</i> with 28 passengers on board (despite a capacity of 65). Lifeboat No. 6, on the port side, was the next to be lowered at 00:55. It also had 28 people on board, among them the "unsinkable" <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Brown" title="Margaret Brown">Margaret "Molly" Brown</a>. Lightoller realised there was only one seaman on board (Quartermaster Robert Hitchens) and called for volunteers. Major <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Godfrey_Peuchen" title="Arthur Godfrey Peuchen">Arthur Godfrey Peuchen</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Yacht_Club" title="Royal Canadian Yacht Club">Royal Canadian Yacht Club</a> stepped forward and climbed down a rope into the lifeboat; he was the only male passenger whom Lightoller allowed to board during the port side evacuation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord197687_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord197687-99"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> Peuchen's role highlighted a key problem during the evacuation: there were hardly any seamen to man the boats. Some had been sent below to open gangway doors to allow more passengers to be evacuated, but they never returned. They were presumably trapped and drowned by the rising water below decks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011150_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011150-100"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, other crewmen fought for their lives as water continued to pour into the ship below decks. The engineers and firemen worked to vent steam from the boilers to prevent them from exploding on contact with the cold water. They set up extra pumps in the forward compartments in a futile bid to reduce the torrent, and kept the electrical generators running to maintain lights and power throughout the ship. Steward F. Dent Ray narrowly avoided being swept away when a wooden wall between his quarters and the third-class accommodation on E deck collapsed, leaving him waist-deep in water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord197678_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord197678-101"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Two engineers died in boiler room No. 5 when, at around 00:45, the bunker door separating it from the flooded No. 6 boiler room collapsed and they were swept away by "a wave of green foam".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011126_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011126-102"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In boiler room No. 4, at around 01:20, water began flooding in from below, possibly indicating that the bottom of the ship had also been holed by the iceberg. The flow of water soon overwhelmed the pumps and forced the firemen and trimmers to evacuate the forward boiler rooms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord197676_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord197676-103"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> Further <a href="/wiki/Aft" title="Aft">aft</a>, Chief Engineer William Bell, his engineering colleagues, and a handful of volunteer firemen and greasers stayed behind in the unflooded No. 1, 2 and 3 boiler rooms and in the turbine and reciprocating engine rooms. They continued working on the boilers and the electrical generators in order to keep the ship's lights on and to power the radio so that distress signals could be sent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198725_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198725-47"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> They apparently remained at their posts until the very end, thus ensuring that <i>Titanic</i> remained lit until the final minutes of the sinking. None of the ship's 35 engineers and electricians survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998226_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998226-104"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> Neither did any of the <i>Titanic</i> '​s five postal clerks, who were last seen struggling to save the mail bags they had rescued from the flooded mail room. They were caught by the rising water somewhere on D deck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998225_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998225-105"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many of the third-class passengers were also confronted with the sight of water pouring into their quarters on E, F and G decks. Carl Jansson, one of the relatively small number of third-class survivors, later recalled:</p>
<dl>
<dd>Then I run down to my cabin to bring my other clothes, watch and bag but only had time to take the watch and coat when water with enormous force came into the cabin and I had to rush up to the deck again where I found my friends standing with lifebelts on and with terror painted on their faces. What should I do now, with no lifebelt and no shoes and no cap?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleicher200640_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleicher200640-106"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>The lifeboats were lowered every few minutes on each side, but most of the boats were greatly under-filled. No. 5 left with 41 aboard, No. 3 had 32 aboard, No. 8 left with 39<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198724_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198724-107"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> and No. 1 left with just 12 out of a capacity of 40.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198724_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198724-107"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> The evacuation did not go smoothly and passengers suffered accidents and injuries as it progressed. One woman fell between lifeboat No. 10 and the side of the ship but someone caught her by the ankle and hauled her back onto the promenade deck, where she made a second successful attempt at boarding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord197690_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord197690-108"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> First-class passenger Annie Stengel broke several ribs when an overweight German-American doctor and his brother jumped into No. 5, squashing her and knocking her unconscious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011147_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011147-109"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994150_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994150-110"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> The lifeboats' descent was likewise risky. No. 6 was nearly flooded during the descent by water discharging out of the ship's side, but successfully made it away from the ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198724_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198724-107"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011145_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011145-111"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> No. 3 came close to disaster when, for a time, one of the davits jammed, threatening to pitch the passengers out of the lifeboat and into the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011152_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011152-112"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">

<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span style="font-size:smaller;"><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
