<p>By 01:20, the seriousness of the situation was now apparent to the passengers above decks, who began saying their goodbyes, with husbands escorting their wives and children to the lifeboats. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distress_rocket" title="Distress rocket">Distress rockets</a> were fired every few minutes to attract the attention of any ships nearby and the radio operators repeatedly sent the <a href="/wiki/Distress_signal" title="Distress signal">distress signal</a> <a href="/wiki/CQD" title="CQD">CQD</a>. Radio operator <a href="/wiki/Harold_Bride" title="Harold Bride">Harold Bride</a> suggested to his colleague Jack Phillips that he should use the new <a href="/wiki/SOS" title="SOS">SOS</a> signal, as it "may be your last chance to send it". The two radio operators contacted other ships to ask for assistance. Several responded, of which RMS <i>Carpathia</i> was the closest, at 58 miles (93 km) away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler199898_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler199898-113"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> She was a much slower vessel than <i>Titanic</i> and, even driven at her maximum speed of 17 <a href="/wiki/Knot_(unit)" title="Knot (unit)">kn</a> (20 mph; 31 km/h), would have taken four hours to reach the sinking ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998113_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998113-114"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Much nearer was the <a href="/wiki/SS_Californian" title="SS Californian">SS <i>Californian</i></a>, which had warned <i>Titanic</i> of ice a few hours earlier. Apprehensive at his ship being caught in a large field of drift ice, the <i>Californian's</i> captain, <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Lord" title="Stanley Lord">Stanley Lord</a>, had decided at about 22:00 to halt for the night and wait for daylight to find a way through the ice field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998159_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998159-115"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> At 23:30, only 10 minutes before <i>Titanic</i> hit the iceberg, <i>Californian</i> '​s sole radio operator, <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Furmstone_Evans" title="Cyril Furmstone Evans">Cyril Evans</a>, shut his set down for the night and went to bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998161_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998161-116"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> On the bridge her Third Officer, Charles Groves, saw a large vessel to starboard around 10 to 12 mi (16 to 19 km) away. It made a sudden turn to port and stopped. If the radio operator of the <i>Californian</i> had stayed at his post fifteen minutes longer, hundreds of lives might have been saved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998160_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998160-117"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> A little over an hour later, Second Officer Herbert Stone saw five white rockets exploding above the stopped ship. Unsure what the rockets meant, he called Captain Lord, who was resting in the chartroom, and reported the sighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998162_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998162-118"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> Lord did not act on the report, but Stone was perturbed: "A ship is not going to fire rockets at sea for nothing," he told a colleague.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998163_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998163-119"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_signal.jpg"><img alt='Image of a distress signal reading: "SOS SOS CQD CQD. MGY [Titanic]. We are sinking fast passengers being put into boats. MGY"' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="402" data-file-width="500" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Titanic_signal.jpg/220px-Titanic_signal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Titanic_signal.jpg/330px-Titanic_signal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Titanic_signal.jpg/440px-Titanic_signal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_signal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Distress signal sent at about 01:40 by <i>Titanic</i> '​s radio operator, Jack Phillips, to the <a href="/wiki/Russian_American_Line" title="Russian American Line">Russian</a> ship SS <i>Birma</i>. This was one of <i>Titanic</i> '​s last intelligible radio messages.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By this time, it was clear to those on <i>Titanic</i> that the ship was indeed sinking and there would not be enough lifeboat places for everyone. Some still clung to the hope that the worst would not happen: Lucien Smith told his wife, "It is only a matter of form to have women and children first. The ship is thoroughly equipped and everyone on her will be saved."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord197684_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord197684-120"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> Charlotte Colyer's husband Harvey called to his wife as she was put in a lifeboat, "Go, Lottie! For God's sake, be brave and go! I'll get a seat in another boat!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord197684_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord197684-120"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Other couples refused to be separated. <a href="/wiki/Ida_Straus" title="Ida Straus">Ida Straus</a>, the wife of <a href="/wiki/Macy%27s" title="Macy's">Macy's</a> <a href="/wiki/Department_store" title="Department store">department store</a> co-owner <a href="/wiki/Isidor_Straus" title="Isidor Straus">Isidor Straus</a>, told her husband: "We have been living together for many years. Where you go, I go."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord197684_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord197684-120"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> They sat down in a pair of deck chairs and waited for the end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord197685_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord197685-121"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> The industrialist <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Guggenheim" title="Benjamin Guggenheim">Benjamin Guggenheim</a> changed out of his life vest and sweater into top hat and evening dress, and declared his wish to go down with the ship like a gentleman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198725_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198725-47"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At this point, the vast majority of passengers who had boarded lifeboats were from first- and second-class. Few third-class (steerage) passengers had made it up onto the deck, and most were still lost in the maze of corridors or trapped behind barriers and partitions that segregated the accommodation for the steerage passengers from the first- and second-class areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006284_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006284-122"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> This segregation was not simply for social reasons, but was a requirement of United States immigration laws, which mandated that third-class passengers be segregated to control immigration and to prevent the spread of infectious diseases. First- and second-class passengers on transatlantic liners disembarked at the main piers on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manhattan_Island" title="Manhattan Island">Manhattan Island</a>, but steerage passengers had to go through health checks and processing at <a href="/wiki/Ellis_Island" title="Ellis Island">Ellis Island</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowells199996_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowells199996-123"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> In at least some places, <i>Titanic</i> '​s crew appear to have actively hindered the steerage passengers' escape. Some of the barriers were locked and guarded by crew members, apparently to prevent the steerage passengers from rushing the lifeboats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006284_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006284-122"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> Irish survivor Margaret Murphy wrote in May 1912:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Before all the steerage passengers had even a chance of their lives, the <i>Titanic</i> '​s sailors fastened the doors and companionways leading up from the third-class section ... A crowd of men was trying to get up to a higher deck and were fighting the sailors; all striking and scuffling and swearing. Women and some children were there praying and crying. Then the sailors fastened down the hatchways leading to the third-class section. They said they wanted to keep the air down there so the vessel could stay up longer. It meant all hope was gone for those still down there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006284_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006284-122"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>A long and winding route had to be taken to reach topside; the steerage-class accommodation, located on C through G decks, was at the extreme ends of the decks, and so was the farthest away from the lifeboats. By contrast, the first-class accommodation was located on the upper decks and so was nearest. Proximity to the lifeboats thus became a key factor in determining who got in them. To add to the difficulty, many of the steerage passengers did not understand or speak English. It was perhaps no coincidence that English-speaking Irish immigrants were disproportionately represented among the steerage passengers who survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowells199995_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowells199995-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Many of those who did survive owed their lives to third-class steward John Edward Hart, who organised three trips into the ship's interior to escort groups of third-class passengers up to the boat deck. Others made their way through open barriers or climbed emergency ladders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord197691.E2.80.935_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord197691.E2.80.935-124"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some, perhaps overwhelmed by it all, made no attempt to escape and stayed in their cabins or congregated in prayer in the third-class dining room.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord197697_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord197697-125"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> Leading Fireman Charles Hendrickson saw crowds of third-class passengers below decks with their trunks and possessions, as if waiting for someone to direct them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011131_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011131-126"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> Psychologist Wynn Craig Wade attributes this to "stoic passivity" produced by generations of being told what to do by social superiors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998225_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998225-105"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> August Wennerström, one of the male steerage passengers to survive, commented later that many of his companions had made no effort to save themselves. He wrote:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Hundreds were in a circle [in the third-class dining saloon] with a preacher in the middle, praying, crying, asking God and Mary to help them. They lay there and yelled, never lifting a hand to help themselves. They had lost their own will power and expected God to do all the work for them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGittinsAkers-JordanBehe2011167_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGittinsAkers-JordanBehe2011167-127"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Launching_of_the_last_lifeboats">Launching of the last lifeboats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Launching of the last lifeboats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leaving_the_sinking_liner.jpg"><img alt="Painting of lifeboats being lowered down the side of Titanic, with one lifeboat about to be lowered on top of another one in the water. A third lifeboat is visible in the background." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1736" data-file-width="1438" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Leaving_the_sinking_liner.jpg/220px-Leaving_the_sinking_liner.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Leaving_the_sinking_liner.jpg/330px-Leaving_the_sinking_liner.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Leaving_the_sinking_liner.jpg/440px-Leaving_the_sinking_liner.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leaving_the_sinking_liner.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lifeboat No. 15 was nearly lowered onto Lifeboat No. 13 (depicted by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dixon_(artist)" title="Charles Dixon (artist)">Charles Dixon</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By 01:30, <i>Titanic</i> '​s downward angle in the water was increasing and the ship was now listing slightly more to port, but not more than 5 degrees. The deteriorating situation was reflected in the messages sent from the ship, which carried a tone of increasing desperation: "We are putting the women off in the boats" at 01:25, "Engine room getting flooded" at 01:35, and at 01:45, "Engine room full up to boilers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198726_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198726-128"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> This was <i>Titanic</i> '​s last intelligible signal, sent as the ship's electrical system began to fail; subsequent messages were jumbled and broken. The two radio operators nonetheless continued sending out distress messages almost to the very end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERegal200534_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERegal200534-129"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The remaining boats were filled much closer to capacity and in an increasing rush. No. 11 was filled with five people more than its rated capacity. As it was lowered, it was nearly flooded by water being pumped out of the ship, but made it safely to the sea. No. 13 narrowly avoided the same problem but those aboard were unable to release the ropes from which the boat had been lowered. It drifted astern, directly under No. 15 as it was being lowered. The ropes were cut in time and both boats made it away safely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994153_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994153-130"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The first signs of panic were seen when a group of passengers attempted to rush port-side lifeboat No. 14 as it was being lowered with 40 people aboard. <a href="/wiki/Harold_Lowe" title="Harold Lowe">Fifth Officer Lowe</a> in charge of the boat fired three warning shots in the air to control the crowd, without causing injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994154_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994154-131"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> No. 16 was lowered five minutes later. Among those aboard was stewardess <a href="/wiki/Violet_Jessop" title="Violet Jessop">Violet Jessop</a>, who would repeat the experience four years later when she survived the sinking of one of <i>Titanic</i> '​s sister ships, <a href="/wiki/HMHS_Britannic" title="HMHS Britannic"><i>Britannic</i></a>, in the First World War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994155_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994155-132"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> Collapsible boat C was launched at 01:40 from a now largely deserted area of the deck, as most of those on deck had moved to the <a href="/wiki/Stern" title="Stern">stern</a> of the ship. It was aboard this boat that White Star chairman and managing director <a href="/wiki/J._Bruce_Ismay" title="J. Bruce Ismay">J. Bruce Ismay</a>, <i>Titanic</i> '​s most controversial survivor, made his escape from the ship, an act later condemned as cowardice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198726_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198726-128"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At 01:45, lifeboat No. 2 was lowered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987222_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987222-133"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> While it was still at deck level, Lightoller had found the boat occupied by a number of men who, he wrote later, "weren't British, nor of the English-speaking race ... [but of] the broad category known to sailors as '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_slur#D" title="Ethnic slur">dagoes</a>'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960296_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960296-134"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> After he evicted them by threatening them with his revolver, he was unable to find enough women and children to fill the boat<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960296_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960296-134"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> and lowered it with only 25 people on board out of a possible capacity of 40.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987222_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987222-133"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> John Jacob Astor saw his wife off to safety in No. 4 boat at 01:55 but was refused entry by Lightoller, even though 20 of the 60 seats aboard were unoccupied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987222_133-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987222-133"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The last boat to be launched was collapsible D, which left at 02:05 with only 25 people aboard;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bright_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-bright-135"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> two more men jumped on the boat as it was being lowered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> The sea had reached the boat deck and the forecastle was deep underwater. First class passenger Edith Evans gave up her place in the boat, and ultimately died in the disaster. She was one of only four women in first class to perish in the sinking. Captain Smith carried out a final tour of the deck, telling the radio operators and other crew members: "Now it's every man for himself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998130_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998130-137"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As passengers and crew headed to the stern, where Father <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Byles" title="Thomas Byles">Thomas Byles</a> was giving absolutions and hearing confessions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998135_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998135-138"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Titanic</i> '​s band continued to play outside the gymnasium. Part of the enduring folklore of the <i>Titanic</i> sinking is that the band played the hymn "<a href="/wiki/Nearer,_My_God,_to_Thee" title="Nearer, My God, to Thee">Nearer, My God, to Thee</a>" as the ship sank, but this appears to be dubious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowells1999128_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowells1999128-139"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> The claim surfaced among the earliest reports of the sinking,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowells1999129_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowells1999129-140"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> and the hymn became so closely associated with the <i>Titanic</i> disaster that its opening bars were carved on the grave monument of <i>Titanic</i> '​s bandmaster, <a href="/wiki/Wallace_Hartley" title="Wallace Hartley">Wallace Hartley</a>, one of those who perished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichards2001395_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichards2001395-141"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> Violet Jessop said in her 1934 account of the disaster that she had heard the hymn being played.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowells1999128_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowells1999128-139"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> In contrast, Archibald Gracie emphatically denied it in his own account, written soon after the sinking, and Radio Operator Harold Bride said that he had heard "Autumn",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichards2001396_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichards2001396-142"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> by which he may have meant <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Joyce" title="Archibald Joyce">Archibald Joyce</a>'s then-popular waltz "Songe d'Automne" (Autumn Dream). George Orrell, the bandmaster of the rescue ship, <i>Carpathia</i>, who spoke with survivors, related: "The ship's band in any emergency is expected to play to calm the passengers. After <i>Titanic</i> struck the iceberg the band began to play bright music, dance music, comic songs – anything that would prevent the passengers from becoming panic-stricken ... various awe-stricken passengers began to think of the death that faced them and asked the bandmaster to play hymns. The one which appealed to all was 'Nearer My God to Thee'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETurner2011194_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETurner2011194-143"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Gracie, who was near the band until that section of deck went under, the tunes played by the band were "cheerful" but that he didn't recognise any of them, claiming that if they had played 'Nearer, My God, to Thee' as claimed in the newspaper "I assuredly should have noticed it and regarded it as a tactless warning of immediate death to us all and one likely to create panic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGracie191320_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGracie191320-144"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Bride heard the band as he left the radio cabin, which was by now awash, in the company of the other radio operator, Jack Phillips. He had just had a fight with a man who Bride thought was "a stoker, or someone from below decks", who had attempted to steal Phillips' lifebelt. Bride wrote later: "I did my duty. I hope I finished [the man]. I don't know. We left him on the cabin floor of the radio room, and he was not moving."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960317_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960317-145"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> The two radio operators went in opposite directions, Phillips aft and Bride forward towards collapsible lifeboat B.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960317_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960317-145"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Archibald Gracie was also heading aft, but as he made his way towards the stern he found his path blocked by "a mass of humanity several lines deep, covering the boat deck, facing us"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960138.E2.80.939_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960138.E2.80.939-146"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> – hundreds of steerage passengers, who had finally made it to the deck just as the last lifeboats departed. He gave up on the idea of going aft and jumped into the water to get away from the crowd.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960138.E2.80.939_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960138.E2.80.939-146"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> Others made no attempt to escape. The ship's designer, Thomas Andrews, was last seen in the first-class smoking room, having removed his lifebelt, staring at the painting above the fireplace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994155_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994155-132"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChirnside2004177_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChirnside2004177-147"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> Captain Smith's fate is unclear: there are conflicting accounts of his death, he either entered the wheelhouse on the bridge and died there when it was engulfed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011224_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011224-148"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198740.E2.80.9341_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198740.E2.80.9341-149"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> or jumped into the water just before the bridge was submerged and subsequently perished in the water, possibly near Collapsible B.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANTR_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-ANTR-155"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_minutes_of_sinking_.2802:15.E2.80.9302:20.29">Last minutes of sinking (02:15–02:20)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Last minutes of sinking (02:15–02:20)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nearer_My_God_To_Thee_Titanic_-_no_caption.png"><img alt="Cartoon depicting a man standing with a woman, who is hiding her head on his shoulder, on the deck of a ship awash with water. A beam of light is shown coming down from heaven to illuminate the couple. Behind them is an empty davit." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="672" data-file-width="623" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Nearer_My_God_To_Thee_Titanic_-_no_caption.png/220px-Nearer_My_God_To_Thee_Titanic_-_no_caption.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Nearer_My_God_To_Thee_Titanic_-_no_caption.png/330px-Nearer_My_God_To_Thee_Titanic_-_no_caption.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Nearer_My_God_To_Thee_Titanic_-_no_caption.png/440px-Nearer_My_God_To_Thee_Titanic_-_no_caption.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nearer_My_God_To_Thee_Titanic_-_no_caption.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>"Nearer, My God, To Thee"</i> – cartoon of 1912</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At about 02:15, <i>Titanic</i> '​s angle in the water began to increase rapidly as water poured into previously unflooded parts of the ship through deck hatches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarratt2010131_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarratt2010131-156"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> Her suddenly increasing angle caused what one survivor called a "giant wave" to wash along the ship from the forward end of the boat deck, sweeping many people into the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynch1998117_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynch1998117-157"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> The parties who were trying to lower collapsible boats A and B, including Chief Officer Henry Wilde, First Officer Murdoch, Second Officer Charles Lightoller and <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Gracie_IV" title="Archibald Gracie IV">Colonel Archibald Gracie</a>, were swept away along with the two boats (boat B floated away upside-down with Harold Bride trapped underneath it, and boat A ended up partly flooded and with its canvas not raised). Bride, Gracie and Lightoller made it onto boat B, but Murdoch and Wilde perished in the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGracie191361_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGracie191361-158"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960316_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960316-159"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Lightoller opted to abandon his post to escape the growing crowds, and dived into the water from the roof of the officers' quarters. He was sucked into the mouth of a ventilation shaft but was blown clear by "a terrific blast of hot air" and emerged next to the capsized lifeboat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960299_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960299-160"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> The forward funnel collapsed under its own weight, crushing several people as it fell into the water and only narrowly missing the lifeboat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200628_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200628-161"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> It closely missed Lightoller and created a wave that washed the boat 50 yards (46 m) clear of the sinking ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960299_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinocour1960299-160"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> Those still on <i>Titanic</i> felt her structure shuddering as it underwent immense stresses. As first-class passenger Jack Thayer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord2005166_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord2005166-162"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> described it:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Occasionally there had been a muffled thud or deadened explosion within the ship. Now, without warning she seemed to start forward, moving forward and into the water at an angle of about fifteen degrees. This movement with the water rushing up toward us was accompanied by a rumbling roar, mixed with more muffled explosions. It was like standing under a steel railway bridge while an express train passes overhead mingled with the noise of a pressed steel factory and wholesale breakage of china.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleicher2006229_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleicher2006229-163"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Eyewitnesses saw <i>Titanic</i> '​s stern lifting high into the air as the ship tilted down in the water. It was said to have reached an angle of 30–45 degrees,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987202_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987202-164"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> "revolving apparently around a centre of gravity just astern of midships," as Lawrence Beesley later put it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeesley196047_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeesley196047-165"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> Many survivors described a great noise, which some attributed to the boilers exploding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMowbray191270_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMowbray191270-166"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> Beesley described it as "partly a groan, partly a rattle, and partly a smash, and it was not a sudden roar as an explosion would be: it went on successively for some seconds, possibly fifteen to twenty". He attributed it to "the engines and machinery coming loose from their bolts and bearings, and falling through the compartments, smashing everything in their way".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeesley196047_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeesley196047-165"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After another minute, the ship's lights flickered once and then permanently went out, plunging <i>Titanic</i> into darkness. Jack Thayer recalled seeing "groups of the fifteen hundred people still aboard, clinging in clusters or bunches, like swarming bees; only to fall in masses, pairs or singly as the great afterpart of the ship, two hundred fifty feet of it, rose into the sky."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200628_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200628-161"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reuterdahl_-_Sinking_of_the_Titanic.jpg"><img alt="Painting of a sinking ship with a lifeboat being rowed away from it in the foreground." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="798" data-file-width="1010" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Reuterdahl_-_Sinking_of_the_Titanic.jpg/220px-Reuterdahl_-_Sinking_of_the_Titanic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Reuterdahl_-_Sinking_of_the_Titanic.jpg/330px-Reuterdahl_-_Sinking_of_the_Titanic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Reuterdahl_-_Sinking_of_the_Titanic.jpg/440px-Reuterdahl_-_Sinking_of_the_Titanic.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reuterdahl_-_Sinking_of_the_Titanic.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>"Sinking of the Titanic"</i> by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Reuterdahl" title="Henry Reuterdahl">Henry Reuterdahl</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Titanic</i> was subjected to extreme opposing forces – the flooded bow pulling her down while the air in the stern kept her to the surface – which were concentrated at one of the weakest points in the structure, the area of the engine room hatch. Shortly after the lights went out, the ship split apart. The submerged bow may have remained attached to the stern by the keel for a short time, pulling the stern to a high angle before separating and leaving the stern to float for a few minutes longer. The forward part of the stern would have flooded very rapidly, causing it to tilt until it reached its final near-vertical position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011119_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011119-167"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> The stern began to settle back before rising again to a nearly vertical 90 degree angle, where it remained for a few moments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200629_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200629-168"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> The ship disappeared from view at 02:20, 2 hours and 40 minutes after striking the iceberg. Thayer reported that it rotated on the surface, "gradually [turning] her deck away from us, as though to hide from our sight the awful spectacle ... Then, with the deadened noise of the bursting of her last few gallant bulkheads, she slid quietly away from us into the sea."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198729_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198729-169"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Titanic</i> '​s surviving officers and a number of prominent survivors later testified that the ship had sunk in one piece, a belief that was affirmed by the British and American inquiries into the disaster. Archibald Gracie, who was on the promenade deck with the band (by the second funnel), stated that "<i>Titanic</i> '​s decks were intact at the time she sank, and when I sank with her, there was over seven-sixteenths of the ship already under water, and there was no indication then of any impending break of the deck or ship".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGracie191358_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGracie191358-170"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> However, Ballard argued that many other survivors' accounts indicated that the ship had broken in two as it was sinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987201_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987201-171"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup> As the engines are now known to have stayed in place along with most of the boilers, the "great noise" heard by witnesses and the momentary settling of the stern were presumably caused by the break-up of the ship rather than the loosening of her fittings or boiler explosions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuntz1998xiii_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuntz1998xiii-172"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After they went under, the bow and stern took only a few minutes to sink 3,795 metres (12,451 ft), spilling a trail of heavy machinery, tons of coal and large quantities of debris from <i>Titanic</i> '​s interior. The two parts of the ship landed about 600 metres (2,000 ft) apart on a gently undulating area of the seabed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUchupiBallardLange1986_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUchupiBallardLange1986-173"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup> The streamlined bow section continued to descend at about the angle it had taken on the surface, striking the seabed prow-first at a shallow angle<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987206_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987206-174"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup> at an estimated speed of 25–30 mph (40–48 km/h). Its momentum caused it to dig a deep gouge into the seabed and buried the section up to 20 metres (66 ft) deep in sediment before it came to an abrupt halt. The sudden deceleration caused the bow's structure to buckle downwards by several degrees just forward of the bridge. The decks at the rear end of the bow section, which had already been weakened during the break-up, collapsed one atop another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987205_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987205-175"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The stern section seems to have descended almost vertically, probably rotating as it fell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987206_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987206-174"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup> Empty tanks and <a href="/wiki/Cofferdam" title="Cofferdam">cofferdams</a> imploded as it descended, tearing open the structure and ripping off the <a href="/wiki/Poop_deck" title="Poop deck">poop deck</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998140_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998140-176"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> The section landed with such force that it buried itself about 15 metres (49 ft) deep at the rudder. The decks pancaked down on top of each other and the hull plating splayed out to the sides. Debris continued to rain down across the seabed for several hours after the sinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987205_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987205-175"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Passengers_and_crew_in_the_water_.2802:20.E2.80.9304:10.29">Passengers and crew in the water (02:20–04:10)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Passengers and crew in the water (02:20–04:10)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_watch.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of a brass pocket watch on a stand, with a silver chain curled around the base. The watch's hands read 2:28." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="623" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Titanic_watch.jpg/220px-Titanic_watch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Titanic_watch.jpg/330px-Titanic_watch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Titanic_watch.jpg/440px-Titanic_watch.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_watch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pocket watch retrieved from an unknown victim of the disaster, stopped showing a time of 2:28</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gracie.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of a moustached middle-aged man in a dark suit and waistcoat, sitting in a chair while looking at the camera" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="338" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Gracie.jpg/220px-Gracie.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Gracie.jpg/330px-Gracie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/83/Gracie.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gracie.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Archibald_Gracie_IV" title="Archibald Gracie IV">Colonel Archibald Gracie</a>, one of the survivors who made it to collapsible lifeboat B. He never recovered from his ordeal and died eight months after the sinking.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the immediate aftermath of the sinking, hundreds of passengers and crew were left dying in the icy sea, surrounded by debris from the ship. <i>Titanic</i> '​s disintegration during her descent to the seabed caused buoyant chunks of debris – timber beams, wooden doors, furniture, panelling and chunks of cork from the bulkheads – to rocket to the surface. These injured and possibly killed some of the swimmers; others used the debris to try to keep themselves afloat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998139_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998139-177"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>With a temperature of 28 °F (−2 °C), the water was lethally cold. Second Officer Lightoller described the feeling of "a thousand knives" being driven into his body as he entered the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998140_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998140-176"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> Some of those in the water would have died almost instantly from <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">heart attacks</a> caused by the sudden stress on their cardiovascular systems. Others progressed through the classic symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Hypothermia" title="Hypothermia">hypothermia</a>: extreme shivering at first, followed by a slowing and weakening pulse as body temperature dropped, before finally losing consciousness and dying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAldridge200856_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAldridge200856-178"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Those in the lifeboats were horrified to hear the sound of what <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Beesley" title="Lawrence Beesley">Lawrence Beesley</a> called "every possible emotion of human fear, despair, agony, fierce resentment and blind anger mingled – I am certain of those – with notes of infinite surprise, as though each one were saying, 'How is it possible that this awful thing is happening to <i>me</i>? That I should be caught in this death trap?<span style="padding-right:.2em;">'</span>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarratt2010199.E2.80.93200_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarratt2010199.E2.80.93200-179"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jack_Thayer" title="Jack Thayer">Jack Thayer</a> compared it to the sound of "locusts on a summer night", while George Rheims, who jumped moments before <i>Titanic</i> sank, described it as "a dismal moaning sound which I won't ever forget; it came from those poor people who were floating around, calling for help. It was horrifying, mysterious, supernatural."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarratt2010177_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarratt2010177-180"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The noise of the people in the water screaming, yelling, and crying was a tremendous shock to the occupants of the lifeboats, many of whom had up to that moment believed that everyone had escaped before the ship sank. As Beesley later wrote, the cries "came as a thunderbolt, unexpected, inconceivable, incredible. No one in any of the boats standing off a few hundred yards away can have escaped the paralysing shock of knowing that so short a distance away a tragedy, unbelievable in its magnitude, was being enacted, which we, helpless, could in no way avert or diminish."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarratt2010199.E2.80.93200_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarratt2010199.E2.80.93200-179"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Only a few of those in the water survived. Among them were Archibald Gracie, Jack Thayer and Charles Lightoller, who made it to the capsized collapsible boat B. Around 12 crew members climbed on board Collapsible B, and they rescued those they could until some 35 men were clinging precariously to the upturned hull. Realising the risk to the boat of being swamped by the mass of swimmers around them, they paddled slowly away, ignoring the pleas of dozens of swimmers to be allowed on board. In his account, Gracie wrote of the admiration he had for those in the water; "In no instance, I am happy to say, did I hear any word of rebuke from a swimmer because of a refusal to grant assistance... [one refusal] was met with the manly voice of a powerful man... 'All right boys, good luck and God bless you'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGracie191389_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGracie191389-181"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup> Several other swimmers (probably 20 or more) reached Collapsible boat A, which was upright but partly flooded, as its sides had not been properly raised. Its occupants had to sit for hours in a foot of freezing water,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011224_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011224-148"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> and many died of hypothermia during the night.</p>
<p>Farther out, the other eighteen lifeboats – most of which had empty seats – drifted as the occupants debated what, if anything, they should do to rescue the swimmers. No. 4 boat, having remained near the sinking ship, seems to have been closest to the site of the sinking at around 50 metres away; this had enabled two people to drop into the boat and another to be picked up from the water before the ship sank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> After the sinking, seven more men were pulled from the water, though two later died. Collapsible D rescued one male passenger who jumped in the water and swam over to the boat immediately after it had been lowered. In all the other boats, the occupants eventually decided against returning, probably out of fear that they would be capsized in the attempt. Some put their objections more bluntly; Quartermaster Hichens, commanding lifeboat No. 6, told the women aboard his boat that there was no point returning as there were "only a lot of stiffs there."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011226.E2.80.937_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011226.E2.80.937-183"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After about twenty minutes, the cries began to fade as the swimmers lapsed into unconsciousness and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011228_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011228-184"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> Fifth Officer Lowe, in charge of No. 14 lifeboat, "waited until the yells and shrieks had subsided for the people to thin out" before mounting an attempt to rescue those in the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011230_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011230-185"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup> He gathered together five of the lifeboats and transferred the occupants between them to free up space in No. 14. Lowe then took a crew of seven crewmen and one male passenger who volunteered to help, and then rowed back to the site of the sinking. The whole operation took about three-quarters of an hour. By the time No. 14 headed back to the site of the sinking, almost all of those in the water were already dead and only a few voices could still be heard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998144.E2.80.935_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998144.E2.80.935-186"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lucy,_Lady_Duff-Gordon" title="Lucy, Lady Duff-Gordon">Lucy, Lady Duff-Gordon</a>, recalled after the disaster that "the very last cry was that of a man who had been calling loudly: 'My God! My God!' He cried monotonously, in a dull, hopeless way. For an entire hour there had been an awful chorus of shrieks, gradually dying into a hopeless moan, until this last cry that I speak of. Then all was silent."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEverett1912167_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEverett1912167-187"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup> Lowe and his crew found four men still alive, one of whom died shortly afterwards. Otherwise, all they could see were "hundreds of bodies and lifebelts"; the dead "seemed as if they had perished with the cold as their limbs were all cramped up."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011230_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011230-185"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the other boats, there was nothing the survivors could do but await the arrival of rescue ships. The air was bitterly cold and several of the boats had taken on water. The survivors could not find any <a href="/wiki/Emergency_rations" title="Emergency rations">food or drinkable water</a> in the boats, and most had no lights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011232_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011232-188"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> The situation was particularly bad aboard collapsible B, which was only kept afloat by a diminishing air pocket in the upturned hull. As dawn approached, the wind rose and the sea became increasingly choppy, forcing those on the collapsible boat to stand up to balance it. Some, exhausted by the ordeal, fell off into the sea and were drowned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011231_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011231-189"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup> It became steadily harder for the rest to keep their balance on the hull, with waves washing across it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011238_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011238-190"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> Archibald Gracie later wrote of how he and the other survivors sitting on the upturned hull were struck by "the utter helplessness of our position."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGracie1913161_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGracie1913161-191"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup> Some swimmers who had reached collapsible A had not strength enough to come aboard, and had to cling to the boat's sides. The bodies of the majority of the people who died during the night were lowered into the sea to make more room for the survivors.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rescue_and_departure_.2804:10.E2.80.9309:15.29">Rescue and departure (04:10–09:15)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Rescue and departure (04:10–09:15)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_lifeboat.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of a lifeboat, filled with people wearing life jackets, being rowed towards the camera." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1880" data-file-width="2759" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Titanic_lifeboat.jpg/220px-Titanic_lifeboat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Titanic_lifeboat.jpg/330px-Titanic_lifeboat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Titanic_lifeboat.jpg/440px-Titanic_lifeboat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_lifeboat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Collapsible lifeboat D photographed from the deck of <i>Carpathia</i> on the morning of 15 April 1912.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Titanic</i> '​s survivors were finally rescued around 04:00 on 15 April by the RMS <i>Carpathia</i>, which had steamed through the night at high speed and at considerable risk, as the ship had to dodge numerous icebergs <i>en route</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011238_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011238-190"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Carpathia</i> '​s lights were first spotted around 03:30,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011238_190-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011238-190"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> which greatly cheered the survivors, though it took several more hours for everyone to be brought aboard. The 30 or more men on collapsible B finally managed to board two other lifeboats, but one survivor died just before the transfer was made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011240.E2.80.931_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011240.E2.80.931-192"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> Collapsible A was also in trouble and was now nearly awash; many of those aboard (maybe more than a half) had died overnight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998140_176-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998140-176"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> The remaining survivors – an unknown number of men, estimated to be between 10–11 and more than 20, and one woman – were transferred from A into another lifeboat, leaving behind three bodies in the boat, which was left to drift away. It was recovered a month later by the White Star liner <a href="/wiki/RMS_Oceanic_(1899)" title="RMS Oceanic (1899)"><i>Oceanic</i></a>, with the bodies still aboard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011240.E2.80.931_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011240.E2.80.931-192"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Those on <i>Carpathia</i> were startled by the scene that greeted them as the sun came up: "fields of ice on which, like points on the landscape, rested innumerable pyramids of ice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011242_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011242-193"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup> Captain <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Rostron" title="Arthur Rostron">Arthur Rostron</a> of <i>Carpathia</i> saw ice all around, including 20 large bergs measuring up to 200 feet (61 m) high and numerous smaller bergs, as well as ice floes and debris from <i>Titanic</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011242_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011242-193"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup> It appeared to <i>Carpathia</i> '​s passengers that their ship was in the middle of a vast white plain of ice, studded with icebergs appearing like hills in the distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011245_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011245-194"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As the lifeboats were brought alongside <i>Carpathia</i>, the survivors came aboard the ship by various means. Some were strong enough to climb up rope ladders; others were hoisted up in slings, and the children were hoisted in mail sacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998154_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998154-195"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> The last lifeboat to reach the ship was Lightoller's boat No. 12, with 74 people aboard a boat designed to carry 65. They were all on <i>Carpathia</i> by 09:00.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998156_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998156-196"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> There were some scenes of joy as some families and friends were reunited, but in most cases hopes died as loved ones failed to reappear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998155_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998155-197"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At 09:15, two more ships appeared on the scene – <a href="/wiki/SS_Mount_Temple" title="SS Mount Temple"><i>Mount Temple</i></a> and <i>Californian</i>, which had finally learned of the disaster when her radio operator returned to duty – but by then there were no more survivors to be rescued. <i>Carpathia</i> had been bound for Fiume, <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a>, (now <a href="/wiki/Rijeka" title="Rijeka">Rijeka</a>, Croatia) but as she had neither the stores nor the medical facilities to cater for the survivors, Rostron ordered that a course be calculated to return the ship to New York, where the survivors could be properly looked after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998156_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998156-196"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Carpathia</i> departed the area, leaving the other ships to carry out a final, fruitless, two-hour search.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998157_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998157-198"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011255_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011255-199"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg"><img alt="A man wearing a bowler hat and a woman in a shawl embrace among a crowd of people standing in a wooden building" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1760" data-file-width="1409" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg/220px-Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg/330px-Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg/440px-Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arrival_of_the_ship_of_sorrow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
According to an eyewitness report, there "were many pathetic scenes" when <i>Titanic</i> '​s survivors disembarked at New York.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Carpathia</i> arrived at Pier 34 in New York on the evening of 18 April after a difficult voyage through pack ice, fog, thunderstorms and rough seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011266_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011266-200"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord1976196.E2.80.937_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord1976196.E2.80.937-201"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> Some 40,000 people stood on the waterfront, alerted to the disaster by a stream of radio messages from <i>Carpathia</i> and other ships. Due to communications difficulties, it was only after <i>Carpathia</i> docked – a full three days after <i>Titanic</i> '​s sinking – that the full scope of the disaster became public knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord1976196.E2.80.937_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord1976196.E2.80.937-201"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> The heaviest loss was in Southampton, home to most of the crew; 699 members of the crew gave Southampton addresses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006266_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006266-202"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> and 549 Southampton residents, almost all crew, were lost in the disaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011264_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011264-203"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Even before <i>Carpathia</i> arrived in New York, efforts were getting underway to retrieve the dead. Four ships chartered by the White Star Line succeeded in retrieving 328 bodies; 119 were buried at sea, while the remaining 209 were brought ashore to the Canadian port of <a href="/wiki/Halifax_(former_city)" title="Halifax (former city)">Halifax, Nova Scotia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011266_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011266-200"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup> where 150 of them were buried.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994235_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994235-204"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup> Memorials were raised in various places – New York, Washington, Southampton, Liverpool, Belfast and Lichfield, among others<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994296.E2.80.93300_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994296.E2.80.93300-205"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup> – and ceremonies were held on both sides of the Atlantic to commemorate the dead and raise funds to aid the survivors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994293.E2.80.935_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994293.E2.80.935-206"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup> The bodies of most of <i>Titanic</i> '​s victims were never recovered, and the only evidence of their deaths was found 73 years later among the debris on the seabed: pairs of shoes lying side by side, where bodies had once lain before eventually decomposing in the sea waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198725_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198725-47"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The prevailing public reaction to the disaster was one of shock and outrage, directed against a number of issues and people: why were there so few lifeboats? Why had Ismay saved his own life when so many others died? Why did <i>Titanic</i> proceed into the icefield at full speed?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBj.C3.B6rkfors200459_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBj.C3.B6rkfors200459-207"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> The outrage was driven not least by the survivors themselves; even while they were aboard <i>Carpathia</i> on their way to New York, Beesley and other survivors determined to "awaken public opinion to safeguard ocean travel in the future" and wrote a public letter to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> urging changes to maritime safety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeesley196081_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeesley196081-208"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In places closely associated with <i>Titanic</i>, there was a deep sense of grief. Crowds of weeping women, the wives, sisters and mothers of crew members, gathered outside the White Star Line's offices in Southampton to find out what had happened to their loved ones – most of whom had perished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998173_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998173-209"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup> Churches in Belfast were packed and shipyard workers wept in the streets after the news was announced. The ship had been a symbol of Belfast's industrial achievements and there was not only a sense of grief but also of guilt, as those who had built <i>Titanic</i> came to feel that they had in some way been responsible for her loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006221.E2.80.932_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski2006221.E2.80.932-210"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inquiries">Inquiries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Inquiries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_inquiry_into_the_sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="United States Senate inquiry into the sinking of the RMS Titanic">United States Senate inquiry into the sinking of the RMS Titanic</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Wreck_Commissioner%27s_inquiry_into_the_sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="British Wreck Commissioner's inquiry into the sinking of the RMS Titanic">British Wreck Commissioner's inquiry into the sinking of the RMS Titanic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Changes_in_safety_practices_after_the_sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Changes in safety practices after the sinking of the RMS Titanic">Changes in safety practices after the sinking of the RMS Titanic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Time_To_Get_Busy_-_no_caption.png"><img alt="Cartoon of Uncle Sam taking hold of a ship's wheel marked &quot;Navigation Laws&quot; and saying, &quot;By ginger, I'll take a firmer grip on this business hereafter.&quot; At his feet is a paper reading &quot;Ragged marine regulations&quot;. A worried-looking man in a top hat marked &quot;Steamship Magnate&quot; looks on. In the distant background a ship can be seen sinking." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="611" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Time_To_Get_Busy_-_no_caption.png/220px-Time_To_Get_Busy_-_no_caption.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Time_To_Get_Busy_-_no_caption.png/330px-Time_To_Get_Busy_-_no_caption.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Time_To_Get_Busy_-_no_caption.png/440px-Time_To_Get_Busy_-_no_caption.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Time_To_Get_Busy_-_no_caption.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>"Time to get busy"</i> by "Fisher", 1912. Public outrage at the disaster led politicians to impose new regulations on the shipping industry.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the aftermath of the sinking, public inquiries were set up in Britain and the United States. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_inquiry_into_the_sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="United States Senate inquiry into the sinking of the RMS Titanic">US inquiry</a> began on 19 April under the chairmanship of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Alden_Smith" title="William Alden Smith">William Alden Smith</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998181_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998181-211"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup> while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Titanic_inquiry" title="British Titanic inquiry">British inquiry</a> commenced in London under <a href="/wiki/John_Bigham,_1st_Viscount_Mersey" title="John Bigham, 1st Viscount Mersey">Lord Mersey</a> on 2 May 1912.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998192_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998192-212"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup> They reached broadly similar conclusions: the regulations on the number of lifeboats that ships had to carry were out of date and inadequate;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998195_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998195-213"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup> Captain Smith had failed to take proper heed of ice warnings;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998189_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998189-214"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup> the lifeboats had not been properly filled or crewed; and the collision was the direct result of steaming into a danger area at too high a speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998195_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998195-213"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup> Captain Lord of the <i>Californian</i> was strongly criticised by both inquiries for failing to render assistance to <i>Titanic</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998191.2C_196_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998191.2C_196-215"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>However, neither inquiry found <a href="/wiki/Negligence" title="Negligence">negligence</a> by IMM or the White Star Line as a factor. The US inquiry concluded that those involved had merely followed standard practice, and the disaster could thus only be categorised as an "<a href="/wiki/Act_of_God" title="Act of God">act of God</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200667_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarczewski200667-216"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup> and the British inquiry concluded that Smith had followed long-standing practice which had not previously been shown to be unsafe<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynch1998189_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynch1998189-217"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup> (the inquiry noted that British ships alone had carried 3.5 million passengers over the previous decade with the loss of just 73 lives<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994265_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994265-218"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup>) and concluded that he had done "only that which other skilled men would have done in the same position." The British inquiry also warned that "What was a mistake in the case of the <i>Titanic</i> would without doubt be negligence in any similar case in the future."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynch1998189_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynch1998189-217"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The disaster led to major changes in maritime regulations to implement new safety measures, such as ensuring that more lifeboats were provided, that lifeboat drills were properly carried out and that radio equipment on passenger ships was manned around the clock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987109_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987109-219"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/International_Ice_Patrol" title="International Ice Patrol">International Ice Patrol</a> was set up to monitor the presence of icebergs in the North Atlantic, and maritime safety regulations were harmonised internationally through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Convention_for_the_Safety_of_Life_at_Sea" title="International Convention for the Safety of Life at Sea">International Convention for the Safety of Life at Sea</a> (SOLAS); both measures are still in force today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994310_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994310-220"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_aftermath_and_wreckage">Cultural aftermath and wreckage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cultural aftermath and wreckage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/RMS_Titanic_in_popular_culture" title="RMS Titanic in popular culture">RMS Titanic in popular culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Wreck of the RMS Titanic">Wreck of the RMS Titanic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="700" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg/220px-Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg/330px-Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg/440px-Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wreck of the RMS <i>Titanic</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Titanic'</i>s sinking became a cultural phenomenon, commemorated by numerous artists, film-makers, writers, composers, musicians and dancers from the time immediately after the sinking to the present day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoster199714_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoster199714-221"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup> On 1 September 1985 a joint US-French expedition led by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ballard" title="Robert Ballard">Robert Ballard</a> found the <a href="/wiki/Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Wreck of the RMS Titanic">wreck of <i>Titanic</i></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198782_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198782-222"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup> and the ship's rediscovery led to an explosion of interest in <i>Titanic</i> '​s story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011332_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett2011332-223"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1997, <a href="/wiki/James_Cameron" title="James Cameron">James Cameron</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Titanic_(1997_film)" title="Titanic (1997 film)">eponymous film</a> became the first movie ever to take $1 billion at the box office, and the <a href="/wiki/Titanic:_Music_from_the_Motion_Picture" title="Titanic: Music from the Motion Picture">film's soundtrack</a> became the best selling soundtrack recording of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParisi1998223_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParisi1998223-224"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup> Numerous expeditions have been launched to film the wreck and, controversially, to salvage objects from the debris field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994310_220-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994310-220"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although many artefacts have been recovered and conserved, the wreck itself is steadily decaying, turning to oxide at a typical marine rate of 0.5–1 ton per day (assuming one ten-thousandth of an inch per day on all surfaces).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCartyFoecke2012196.E2.80.93199_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCartyFoecke2012196.E2.80.93199-225"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup> In time, <i>Titanic</i> '​s structure will collapse into a pile of iron and steel fragments. Eventually she will be reduced to a spot of rust on the seabed, with the remaining scraps of the ship's hull mingled with her more durable fittings, like her propellers, the bronze capstans and the telemotor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998235_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998235-226"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Casualties_and_survivors">Casualties and survivors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Casualties and survivors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Passengers_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Passengers of the RMS Titanic">Passengers of the RMS Titanic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crew_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Crew of the RMS Titanic">Crew of the RMS Titanic</a></div>
<p>The number of casualties of the sinking is unclear, due to a number of factors, including confusion over the passenger list, which included some names of people who cancelled their trip at the last minute, and the fact that several passengers travelled under aliases for various reasons and were double-counted on the casualty lists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998239_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998239-227"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup> The death toll has been put at between 1,490 and 1,635 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord1976197_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord1976197-228"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup> The figures below are from the British <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Trade" title="Board of Trade">Board of Trade</a> report on the disaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMersey1912110.E2.80.931_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMersey1912110.E2.80.931-229"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders sortable" style="margin: 1em auto; text-align: right;">
<tr>
<th scope="col">Passengers</th>
<th scope="col">Category</th>
<th scope="col">Number aboard</th>
<th scope="col">Percentage by total onboard</th>
<th scope="col">Number saved</th>
<th scope="col">Number lost</th>
<th scope="col">Percentage saved</th>
